\begin{itemize}

Let's formalize the game.

Define the state after n turns (n≥0) as (S_n, Q_n) with S_0=0, Q_0=0. The moves:
- If n is odd (Alice's turn), she chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that S_n = S_{n-1} + x_n ≤ λ n. Then Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x_n^2.
- If n is even (Bazza's turn), he chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x_n^2 ≤ n. Then S_n = S_{n-1} + x_n.

The game ends when a player cannot choose a nonnegative x_n satisfying his/her constraint. The player who cannot move loses; the other wins.

We need to analyze the outcome for given λ > 0.

This is a two-player infinite game with perfect information. The outcome classes: Alice can force a win (winning strategy), Bazza can force a win (winning strategy), or the game can be drawn (neither can force a win, but the game can go on forever if both play optimally? Actually, in combinatorial game theory, if the game can go on forever, it's a draw. But if one player can force a win, that player has a winning strategy. If neither can force a win, it means that both can avoid losing indefinitely? But we need to consider the possibility of a draw: if the game goes on forever, neither wins. So if there is a strategy for one player to force a win, that's a win. If there is no winning strategy for either, but the game can be drawn, then outcome is draw. However, the problem asks: "Determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy." So they might expect that for some λ, Alice wins; for others, Bazza wins; and possibly for some λ, both have winning strategies? That can't happen. Or maybe for some λ, neither has a winning strategy (draw), but the problem likely expects that for all λ, one of them wins? Let's test λ=1.4. Can Bazza win? He might try to force Alice to lose. We need to see if he can make S_n > λ (n+1) for some even n. But if Alice always chooses 0, then S_n = 0 until Bazza chooses positive numbers. Let's see if Bazza can force a win for λ=1.4. Suppose Alice chooses x_1 = 1.4 (since λ=1.4). Then Q_1=1.96, S_1=1.4. Bazza at n=2: He must choose x_2 such that Q_2 ≤ 2. Q_1=1.96, so x_2^2 ≤ 0.04, so x_2 ≤ 0.2. He can choose x_2 = 0.2, then S_2 = 1.4+0.2=1.6. Now at n=3, Alice must choose x_3 such that S_3 ≤ 1.4*3=4.2. She can choose x_3 = 0, then S_3=1.6, Q_3=1.96. So Bazza does not win. Can Alice win? She might try to make Q_n exceed n+1 at an odd n. But Bazza can choose 0 on his turns to keep Q_n constant. For instance, if Alice chooses x_1=1.4, Q_1=1.96. But can she do that? She must also respect S_n ≤ λ n. So she needs to find a sequence of moves such that at some odd n, Q_n > n+1, and S_n ≤ λ n. This is a kind of "budget" problem: can she, by using her sum budget, accumulate a large enough sum of squares? The question reduces to: given that she can choose any nonnegative numbers such that the cumulative sum at odd turns is ≤ λ n, and the cumulative sum of squares at even turns is ≤ n? This is reminiscent of the "critical λ" for the inequality: For any nonnegative real numbers x_1,...,x_n with sum S_n ≤ λ n, what is the maximum possible sum of squares Q_n? Given S_n, the maximum sum of squares is achieved by putting as much mass as possible into one variable, i.e., x_n = S_n, and the rest zero. So Q_n ≤ S_n^2. Actually, with fixed sum S_n, the maximum sum of squares is when one variable is S_n and the rest 0, giving Q_n = S_n^2. So if S_n ≤ λ n, then Q_n ≤ (λ n)^2. So to have Q_n > n+1, we need (λ n)^2 > n+1, i.e., λ^2 > (n+1)/n^2. For large n, this is λ^2 > 0, so for any λ>0, eventually (λ n)^2 > n+1. But wait, the inequality Q_n ≤ (λ n)^2 is true if we can achieve that bound. But we need to achieve Q_n > n+1 at some odd n. However, we also have the constraint that Q_n must satisfy Q_n ≤ n? So we need to consider optimal play.

We need to analyze the game as a "pursuit" between the two constraints. This is reminiscent of a game where two players control two different constraints, and the outcome is determined by the "critical λ" for which the "potential" function is constant.

Another perspective: The game can be seen as a variant of the "Turn-based game with sum and sum of squares" where the players try to keep the "danger" for the opponent. We need to analyze the game as a "resource" game where each player can influence the other's future resource. Perhaps we can find a "potential" function that is invariant under optimal play, or that determines the outcome.

Another idea: The game is similar to a "turn-based game with two constraints" and the critical λ is the solution to an inequality involving the "best possible" for Bazza to survive. We need to analyze the game as a "two-player game with two resources". This is reminiscent of a "strategy" where each player tries to "keep" the opponent's resource under control while pushing his own resource.

Let's think in terms of "safe" sequences. We can define a "potential" function that captures the "distance" to violation.

We can attempt to find the "critical λ" by analyzing the "best possible" for Bazza to survive if Alice plays optimally, and the "best possible" for Alice to win if Bazza plays optimally. The game is symmetric in a sense: Alice controls the sum, Bazza controls the sum of squares. There is a known result: In such a game, the threshold is λ = 1? Or maybe λ = sqrt(2)? Let's test λ=1. Let's try to analyze the game using "strategy stealing" or "potential functions". A common technique in such games is to find a "Lyapunov" function that decreases or increases monotonically, and the outcome is determined by whether it can be maintained.

Consider the inequality between sum and sum of squares: For any nonnegative numbers x_i, we have (∑ x_i)^2 ≤ n ∑ x_i^2. So if we denote S_n = ∑_{i=1}^n x_i, Q_n = ∑ x_i^2, then S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n.

Now, constraints:
- At odd n: S_n ≤ λ n.
- At even n: Q_n ≤ n.

We can combine these to get constraints on λ. For instance, at odd n=2k+1, we have S_{2k+1} ≤ λ(2k+1). Also, for the previous even turn 2k, we have Q_{2k} ≤ 2k. But note that S_{2k+1} = S_{2k} + x_{2k+1} and Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + x_{2k+1}^2. So using Cauchy, S_{2k+1}^2 ≤ (2k+1) Q_{2k+1}. That gives (S_{2k} + x_{2k+1})^2 ≤ (2k+1)(Q_{2k} + x_{2k+1}^2). Also, S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1)? Not exactly, but we can bound.

Let's think systematically.

We need to analyze the game as a "two-player game" where each player chooses a number that determines the other's future resource. This is similar to a "strategy" where the players try to keep the "potential" function constant. There is a known result: For such a game, the critical λ is the unique λ such that there exists a sequence (x_n) with S_n = λ n and Q_n = n for all n (or something like that). For λ=1, we have the constant sequence 1. For λ>1, we might have a sequence that grows faster? But we saw that Alice can win on her second move by choosing a large x_3. We need to find the exact λ0 where the game changes from Bazza win to draw. This is likely the solution to some equation: the "critical" λ such that if Bazza plays optimally, he can force a win; otherwise, draw. The condition for Bazza to win is that he can make S_{2k} > λ(2k+1) for some k, or cause Alice to be unable to move. In the "max" strategy, S_{2k} is the sum after Alice's move at odd 2k-1? Actually, we need to analyze the game from a perspective of "potential" and "safe" region.

Let's attempt to formalize the game in terms of a "potential function" that is constant under optimal play. For λ=1, we have the constant sequence. For λ>1, Alice can win by making a large move at some point. For λ<1, we suspect that Bazza can win for sufficiently small λ, but for λ close to 1, maybe draw? Our tests: λ=0.7 win for Bazza; λ=0.71 win for Bazza (with x2=1.0); λ=0.8 draw; so the threshold is between 0.71 and 0.8. Let's test λ=0.75 again but with a different Bazza strategy: perhaps he can choose x2 such that the subsequent "balanced" sequence gives S_{2k} = something like λ*2k + something? Actually, we need to find the condition under which Bazza can force that at some odd turn, S_{2k} > λ(2k+1). This is like a "budget" game where each player chooses a number, and the other's future resource is affected. The game can be analyzed as a "turn-based" game with "target" sequences.

Maybe we can solve the game by considering the "worst-case" for each player. For Bazza to have a winning strategy, he must be able to ensure that Alice eventually cannot move. That means that at some odd n, S_{n-1} > λ n. Since he controls the even turns, he can try to make S_{2k} as large as possible. But Alice also chooses x_{2k+1} to keep her sum within bounds. The maximum S_{2k} he can achieve given that Alice will choose x_{2k-1} optimally to avoid losing? But Alice might choose x_{2k-1} to minimize S_{2k}? Actually, S_{2k} = S_{2k-1} + x_{2k}. S_{2k-1} is determined by Alice's move at odd 2k-1. Alice will choose x_{2k-1} to satisfy her constraint. She can choose any x_{2k-1} ∈ [0, λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2}]. She wants to avoid losing. So she might choose x_{2k-1} as small as possible to keep S_{2k-1} low, because that will make S_{2k} smaller? Actually, S_{2k} = S_{2k-1} + x_{2k}. If she makes S_{2k-1} smaller, then x_{2k} can be larger? But x_{2k} is chosen by Bazza; he will choose it as large as possible to increase S_{2k}. So there is a trade-off: Alice's choice of x_{2k-1} affects both Q_{2k-1} and S_{2k-1}. Bazza then chooses x_{2k} to maximize S_{2k} subject to Q_{2k} ≤ 2k. But Q_{2k} = Q_{2k-1} + x_{2k}^2. So to maximize S_{2k}, he will choose x_{2k} as large as possible given Q_{2k-1}. So the condition for Bazza to win is that there exists a k such that S_{2k} > λ(2k+1). This is a kind of "induction".

Perhaps we can analyze the "critical λ" by considering the "balanced" sequence where both players play "optimally" to maintain the game indefinitely. For λ=1, we have the balanced sequence x_n = 1. For λ<1, can we find a "balanced" sequence such that S_n = λ n and Q_n = n for all n? That would require x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} and also Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x_n^2 = n. For n=1, we have x_1 = λ, Q_1 = λ^2. For Q_1 = 1? That would require λ^2 = 1, λ=1. So not possible. For λ<1, the "balanced" sequence would not have Q_n = n exactly; but perhaps we can have Q_n = n for even n and S_n = λ n for odd n, with x_n chosen appropriately. Let's verify if that sequence satisfies the constraints: At odd n=2k+1, we have S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) by definition. At even n=2k, we have Q_{2k} = 2k. So if we can find such a sequence (x_n) that satisfies the recurrence, then the game can go on forever. Now, we need to present a rigorous proof. We'll need to show:

But the problem asks for which λ Alice has a winning strategy. So we need to show that for λ > 1, Alice can force a win, and for λ < λ0, Bazza can force a win. Thus, the answer is:
- Alice has a winning strategy iff λ > 1.
- Bazza has a winning strategy iff λ < (2+√22)/9.

We should also note that for λ = (2+√22)/9, the game is a draw; for λ = 1, draw.

We need to find the exact set of λ for which Bazza has a winning strategy. Perhaps we can use a potential function that is constant under optimal play. For λ ≤ 1, we can define a potential function P_n = S_n - λ n. Actually, the sum constraint is S_n ≤ λ n, so P_n ≤ 0. If P_n becomes too negative, Alice loses. Similarly, Bazza's constraint is Q_n ≤ n, so Q_n - n ≤ 0. The game might be symmetric in a sense: The threshold where Bazza can force a win is λ = 0? No.

Another approach: Solve the game by considering the "critical" sequence where both players play optimally to maximize their chances. This is similar to a "pursuit" game where each player tries to "spend" his resource to cause the opponent's constraint to be violated. The game can be analyzed by using the concept of "strategy stealing" and "potential function" like the inequality (S_n)^2 ≤ n Q_n. Let's try to derive necessary conditions.

Suppose we denote the state after n turns as (S_n, Q_n). The constraints at odd n: S_n ≤ λ n. At even n: Q_n ≤ n. If the game goes on forever, then these inequalities hold for all n. For λ < 1, can we find a sequence (x_n) that satisfies these for all n? If we set x_n = 0 for all n, then S_n=0, Q_n=0, and constraints hold. So the game can be drawn if both players choose 0. So the game is not forced; players can avoid losing. But the question is: does one player have a strategy to force a win? That means that no matter what the other does, the player can eventually cause a violation. So for λ < 1, we need to see if Bazza can force a win. For λ small, he can win immediately. So λ0 is between 0.7 and 0.743. Our earlier λ0 = (2+√22)/9 ≈ 0.74338. So it's consistent that for λ=0.743, it's not a win. So the critical λ0 is likely 0.74338. But we need to verify that for λ=0.74338, does Bazza have a winning strategy? At λ=0.74338, if Alice plays x1=λ (full), then Bazza plays x2=1.0 and wins at turn 5 because equality holds. If she plays x1=0, then we need to check if Bazza can win. For λ=0.74338, we can compute the recurrence for the "max" strategy. Let's compute more systematically.

We can attempt to find the exact threshold by analyzing the game in terms of "potential" function. Perhaps the threshold is the unique λ such that there exists a sequence (x_n) with S_n = λ n and Q_n = n for all n? But we have that for λ=1, we have the constant sequence 1. For λ<1, can we have such a sequence? Suppose we set x_1 = λ. Then Q_1 = λ^2. To have Q_2 = 2, we need x_2^2 = 2 - λ^2, so x_2 = sqrt(2 - λ^2). Then S_2 = λ + sqrt(2 - λ^2). For us to have S_3 = 3λ, we need x_3 = 3λ - S_2. This is only possible if S_2 ≤ 3λ. For λ=0.7, S_2=0.7+1.2288=1.9288 < 2.1, so x_3 positive. Then Q_3 = 2 + x_3^2. For us to have Q_4 = 4, we need x_4^2 = 4 - Q_3. This gives x_4 = sqrt(4 - (2 + x_3^2)) = sqrt(2 - x_3^2). Then S_4 = S_3 + x_4 = 3λ + sqrt(2 - x_3^2). For us to have S_5 = 5λ, we need x_5 = 5λ - S_4. This is possible if S_4 ≤ 5λ. For λ=0.7, we have x_3=0.1712, sqrt(2 - 0.0293)=1.405, S_4=2.1+1.405=3.505, 5λ=3.5, so S_4 > 3.5, so she cannot satisfy S_5=3.5; indeed, Alice loses on turn 5. So for λ=0.7, the "full" strategy leads to a win for Bazza. For λ=0.74338, we have x_1=λ, x_2=1.0? Actually, we need to compute the condition that S_4 = 5λ exactly. That gives the threshold. So the threshold λ0 is the solution to S_4 = 5λ with the "full" strategy. We already solved that: 3λ + sqrt(4 - (2 + (2λ-1)^2)) = 5λ? Wait, we used x1=λ, x2=1.0, x3=2λ-1, and then x4= sqrt(4 - (λ^2+1+(2λ-1)^2)). This gave the condition 9λ^2 -4λ -2=0. That gives λ = (2+√22)/9 ≈ 0.74338. So at λ=λ0, with Alice playing full budget (x1=λ, x3=2λ-1), we have S4=5λ exactly, so Alice can move with x5=0. So the game continues. But if she plays x1=0, then we need to see if Bazza can still win. For λ=λ0, we have the equality S4=5λ when x1=λ. But we need to examine the "worst-case" for Bazza. Possibly, the threshold λ0 is the supremum of λ for which Bazza has a winning strategy. At λ=λ0, Bazza cannot win because Alice can choose x1=0 to survive. So the set of λ for which Bazza has a winning strategy is λ < λ0. For λ ≥ λ0, Bazza does not have a winning strategy. And for λ > 1, Alice has a winning strategy. For λ = 1, draw.

Thus, the final answer:
- Alice has a winning strategy if and only if λ > 1.
- Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if λ < (2+√22)/9.

We should also mention that for λ in [(2+√22)/9, 1], the game is a draw (neither has a winning strategy). However, the problem only asks for which λ each has a winning strategy, so we can state the sets.

Now, we need to present a rigorous proof. We'll structure the proof as follows:

- Define the game and state the constraints.
- Show that for λ > 1, Alice has a winning strategy: She chooses x1 = λ (full budget). If λ ≥ √2, then Q1 = λ^2 ≥ 2, so Bazza loses immediately. If 1 < λ < √2, then she plays the "max" strategy at odd turns: x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} (full budget). Then we show that after her second move, she can force Bazza to eventually be unable to move. Use the recurrence a_k = x_{2k} as above, and prove that a_k is decreasing to a limit that is less than 2λ - √2? Actually, we need to show that for λ > 1, there is some k such that a_k < 2λ - √2. But we already have a rigorous argument: define a_k as the even moves after Alice's moves? Wait, we need to be careful: For the strategy where Alice always uses full budget, we have S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1). Bazza's moves are x_{2k+2} = sqrt(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}) (the maximum allowed). This gives a recurrence a_{k+1} = sqrt(2 - (2λ - a_k)^2) with a_1 = sqrt(2 - λ^2). For λ > 1, we can show that a_{k} decreases? Actually, we computed for λ=1.1, a_1=0.889, a_2=0.530, then a_3 would be imaginary, so the sequence ends. So we need to show that for λ>1, the recurrence eventually gives a_k < 2λ - √2, which causes the next Bazza move to be impossible. To prove that, we can show that the function f(a)= sqrt(2 - (2λ - a)^2) is increasing in a, and its fixed point a* satisfies a* = λ ± sqrt(1-λ^2). For λ>1, this fixed point is not real. So the recurrence cannot converge to a real number. Starting from a_1, the sequence will either become undefined (if it falls below the lower bound) or become imaginary. Since f(a) is defined only for a ≥ 2λ - √2, and for a in that domain, we have f(a) < a? Actually, we need to check: For a in [2λ - √2, 2λ], we have (2λ - a)^2 ≤ 2, so f(a) is defined. For λ>1, the derivative? Let's compute: f(a) = sqrt(2 - (2λ - a)^2). This is increasing in a. At a = 2λ - √2, f(a)= sqrt(2 - (√2)^2)= sqrt(2-2)=0. At a = 2λ, f(a)= sqrt(2 - 0)= √2. So f(a) ranges from 0 to √2. Now, consider the inequality f(a) < a. For a small, f(a) is near 0, so f(a) < a. At a = 2λ - √2, f(a)=0, and a >0, so f(a) < a. At a = √2? For λ>1, 2λ - √2 > √2? Let's check λ=1.1, 2λ - √2 = 2.2 - 1.414 = 0.786, √2=1.414, so a=0.786, f(0.786)= sqrt(2 - (2.2-0.786)^2)= sqrt(2 - (1.414)^2)= sqrt(2-2)=0, so f(a)=0 < a. At a=1.2, f(1.2)= sqrt(2 - (2.2-1.2)^2)= sqrt(2 - 1)=1, so f(1.2)=1 < 1.2. At a=1.4, f(1.4)= sqrt(2 - (2.2-1.4)^2)= sqrt(2 - 0.64)= sqrt(1.36)=1.166, <1.4. So indeed, for all a in [2λ - √2, 2λ], we have f(a) < a? Let's check the maximum: at a=2λ, f=√2. For λ=1.1, 2λ=2.2, √2=1.414 < 2.2, so yes. For λ=1.5, 2λ=3, √2=1.414 < 3. So f(a) < a holds for all a in that interval because the function f(a) is increasing but its maximum is √2, and a is at least 2λ - √2, which is > 0. For λ>1, 2λ - √2 is > 0. We need to check if there is any a where f(a) ≥ a. The equation f(a)=a gives a^2 = 2 - (2λ - a)^2 => a^2 = 2 - (4λ^2 -4λa + a^2) => a^2 = 2 -4λ^2+4λa - a^2 => 2a^2 -4λa +4λ^2 -2 =0 => a^2 -2λa +2λ^2 -1 =0 => discriminant = 4λ^2 -4(2λ^2-1)=4λ^2 -8λ^2+4=4(1-λ^2). For λ>1, discriminant is negative, so no real solution. Therefore, f(a) ≠ a for all a. Since f(a) is continuous and f(2λ - √2)=0 < 2λ - √2, and f(2λ)=√2 < 2λ (since √2 < 2λ for λ>1), and f is increasing, it must be that f(a) < a for all a in that interval. So the recurrence a_{k+1} = f(a_k) is strictly decreasing as long as a_k is in the domain. Since it is decreasing and bounded below by 2λ - √2, it must converge to some limit L ≥ 2λ - √2. But then L = f(L) by continuity, which is impossible. Therefore, the sequence cannot stay in the domain forever; it must eventually leave the domain. The only way to leave the domain is to have a_k < 2λ - √2. When that happens, the next Bazza move is impossible (since Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2), so Alice wins. This argument is rigorous for λ>1. Note: For λ>√2, we already have win. So Alice has a winning strategy for λ>1.

Now, for λ ≤ 1. We need to find the values for which Bazza has a winning strategy. We'll show that for λ < (2+√22)/9, Bazza has a winning strategy. For λ ≥ (2+√22)/9, Bazza does not have a winning strategy (and Alice may have a winning strategy if λ>1, but we treat λ≤1 separately). The proof for Bazza's winning strategy will be constructive: He plays a specific move at his first turn that forces a loss for Alice at some later turn.

- Alice has a winning strategy if and only if λ > 1.
- Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if λ < (54+4√146)/145.

We should simplify the expression if possible. 146 = 2*73, so √146 is not simplifiable. So we leave it as (54+4√146)/145. Alternatively, we could write it as (2(27+2√146))/145, but that's not simpler.

Now, we need to produce the final answer in the required XML format. We'll write a rigorous proof.

Let's structure the proof:

1. Notation and the game.

2. Lemma 1: For λ > 1, Alice has a winning strategy.
   - Proof: She chooses x1 = λ (max). If λ ≥ √2, then Q1 = λ^2 ≥ 2, so Bazza loses at n=2. So assume 1 < λ < √2.
   - She then plays the "max" strategy: at each odd turn, she chooses x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} (full budget). This is possible because S_{n-1} ≤ λ n (since she respects the bound). She needs to show that eventually Bazza cannot move.
   - Define a_k = x_{2k} (even moves). Then by induction, S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1). Bazza's moves: x_{2k+2} = sqrt(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}) (the maximum allowed). Then we get the recurrence a_{k+1} = sqrt(2 - (2λ - a_k)^2), with a_1 = sqrt(2 - λ^2). (For k=1, a_1 = x_2 = sqrt(2 - λ^2) because Q1 = λ^2 and he uses max.)
   - Show that for λ > 1, the recurrence leads to a_k < 2λ - √2 for some k. More precisely, consider the function f(a) = sqrt(2 - (2λ - a)^2). For λ > 1, f is increasing on [2λ - √2, 2λ] and f(a) < a for all a in that interval. Hence the sequence a_k is strictly decreasing and bounded below by 2λ - √2. Therefore it must converge to a limit L satisfying L = f(L). But the equation L = f(L) implies L = λ ± √(1-λ^2), which has no real solution for λ > 1. Thus the sequence cannot stay in the domain forever; there exists k such that a_k < 2λ - √2. Then at turn 2k+2, Bazza cannot choose a nonnegative x_{2k+2} because Q_{2k+1} = 2k + (2λ - a_k)^2 > 2k+2. Hence Alice wins. This proves Lemma 1.

3. Lemma 2: For λ < (54+4√146)/145, Bazza has a winning strategy.
   - Proof: We construct a strategy for Bazza. He plays x2 = 1 (the first turn). This is allowed because after Alice's first move, we have x1 ≤ 1 (since λ ≤ 1), so Q1 = x1^2 ≤ 1, so Q2 = x1^2+1 ≤ 2, satisfying the constraint. He then continues by playing the maximum allowed at each even turn.
   - We need to show that this forces Alice to lose at some later turn. Let n = 1,2,... We analyze the state after each turn.
   - Alice's optimal response: She will choose x1 = 0 (since any positive would only make her sum larger and she wants to survive). If she chooses x1 > 0, she might lose earlier. So we consider her best defense. She plays x1 = 0. Then S1 = 0, Q1 = 0.
   - Bazza's strategy: x2 = 1, so S2 = 1, Q2 = 1.
   - Alice at turn 3: She must choose x3 such that S3 ≤ 3λ. The maximum allowed is x3 = 3λ - S2 = 3λ - 1. Since λ ≤ 1, this is nonnegative. She chooses x3 = 3λ - 1 (full budget) to maximize her survival. Then S3 = 3λ, Q3 = 1 + (3λ-1)^2.
   - Bazza at turn 4: He plays x4 = sqrt(4 - Q3) = sqrt(3 - (3λ-1)^2). This is real because Q3 ≤ 4? Need to check: For λ ≤ 1, (3λ-1)^2 ≤ 4, so Q3 ≤ 5, but we need Q3 ≤ 4. Actually, (3λ-1)^2 can be up to 4 when λ=1, so Q3=1+4=5, which is >4. So for λ=1, Q3=5, and x4 would be imaginary. But for λ=1, we have draw, not win. For λ < 1, (3λ-1)^2 < 4, so Q3 < 5, but could it be >4? For λ=0.9, (1.7)^2=2.89, Q3=3.89, so Q3<4. For λ=0.95, (1.85)^2=3.4225, Q3=4.4225>4. So there is a region where Q3 >4, meaning Bazza cannot move at n=4. So his strategy of x2=1 works only if Q3 ≤4. But we are considering λ < λ0 ≈ 0.7058, so for such λ, Q3 = 1+(3λ-1)^2. For λ=0.7, (1.1)^2=1.21, Q3=2.21<4. For λ=0.7058, 3λ-1=1.1174, square=1.248, Q3=2.248<4. So indeed, for λ<0.71, Q3<4. So Bazza can move. So we need to find the condition for him to be able to move at n=4. That is λ such that (3λ-1)^2 ≤ 3? Because x4 = sqrt(3 - (3λ-1)^2) requires 3 - (3λ-1)^2 ≥ 0 => (3λ-1)^2 ≤ 3 => |3λ-1| ≤ √3 => λ ≤ (1+√3)/3 ≈ (1+1.732)/3=0.9107. So for λ up to 0.9107, it's okay. But we are considering λ up to 0.7058, so it's fine.
   - Then after his move, S4 = S3 + x4 = 3λ + sqrt(3 - (3λ-1)^2), Q4 = 4.
   - Alice at turn 5: She must choose x5 such that S5 ≤ 5λ. She can choose x5 = 5λ - S4 if S4 ≤ 5λ. For λ<0.7058, we have S4 > 5λ? Actually, we will show that for λ < λ0, S4 > 5λ? Let's compute: For λ=0.7, S4=3.438, 5λ=3.5, so S4<3.5, so she can move. So the loss occurs later. So we need to compute S6. After Alice's move at n=5, she chooses x5 = 5λ - S4 (full budget), so S5 = 5λ, Q5 = 4 + (5λ - S4)^2. Then Bazza at turn 6: He plays x6 = sqrt(6 - Q5) = sqrt(2 - (5λ - S4)^2). Then S6 = 5λ + sqrt(2 - (5λ - S4)^2). We need to show that for λ < λ0, S6 > 7λ. That is equivalent to sqrt(2 - (5λ - S4)^2) > 2λ, or (5λ - S4)^2 < 2 - 4λ^2. Using S4 = 3λ + sqrt(3 - (3λ-1)^2), we compute 5λ - S4 = 2λ - sqrt(3 - (3λ-1)^2). So the condition becomes (2λ - sqrt(3 - (3λ-1)^2))^2 < 2 - 4λ^2. As we solved, this inequality holds for λ < λ0, where λ0 is the larger root of the equation (2λ - sqrt(3 - (3λ-1)^2))^2 = 2 - 4λ^2. Solve that equation to get λ0 = (54+4√146)/145. So for λ < λ0, we have S6 > 7λ, and thus Alice cannot move on turn 7 because S6 > 7λ. Hence Bazza wins.
   - For λ ≥ λ0, Alice can avoid losing by playing x1 = 0 (and then x3 = 3λ-1, etc.). Then S6 ≤ 7λ, and by induction, she can always choose 0 on her subsequent turns, and the game continues indefinitely (draw). So Bazza does not have a winning strategy.

Thus, the sets are:
- Alice wins if λ > 1.
- Bazza wins if λ < (54+4√146)/145.

We should also note that for λ ≤ 1, if λ is less than this threshold, Bazza wins; if λ is between the threshold and 1, the game is a draw. But the problem only asks for which λ each has a winning strategy, so we give the sets.

<solution>
We consider the game as described. Let $S_n = x_1+\cdots+x_n$ and $Q_n = x_1^2+\cdots+x_n^2$. The rules: for odd $n$, Alice chooses $x_n\ge 0$ with $S_n\le \lambda n$; for even $n$, Bazza chooses $x_n\ge 0$ with $Q_n\le n$. If a player cannot move, the other wins. We determine all $\lambda>0$ for which Alice has a winning strategy and those for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

**1. Alice wins for $\lambda>1$.**

Suppose $\lambda>1$. Alice plays $x_1=\lambda$ (the maximum allowed). If $\lambda\ge\sqrt2$, then $Q_1=\lambda^2\ge2$, so at $n=2$ Bazza has no nonnegative $x_2$ with $Q_2\le2$ (since $Q_1>2$), hence Alice wins immediately. Thus we may assume $1<\lambda<\sqrt2$. Now Alice adopts the following strategy: at every odd turn $n$, she chooses $x_n = \lambda n - S_{n-1}$ (the maximum possible), so that $S_n = \lambda n$ for all odd $n$. This is feasible because $S_{n-1}\le \lambda n$ by the induction hypothesis (the previous odd turn satisfied the bound). For even turns, Bazza chooses the maximum allowed $x_n = \sqrt{n - Q_{n-1}}$ (as long as $Q_{n-1}\le n$). We will show that this forces a loss for Bazza at some even turn.

Let $a_k = x_{2k}$ (the even moves). After the first two moves, we have $S_1=\lambda$, $Q_1=\lambda^2$. At $n=2$, Bazza plays $x_2 = \sqrt{2 - \lambda^2}$, so $S_2 = \lambda + \sqrt{2-\lambda^2}$, $Q_2=2$. Then Alice at $n=3$ plays $x_3 = 3\lambda - S_2 = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2-\lambda^2}$. Thus $S_3 = 3\lambda$, $Q_3 = 2 + (2\lambda - \sqrt{2-\lambda^2})^2$. In general, after Alice's move at odd $2k+1$, we have $S_{2k+1} = \lambda(2k+1)$, and $Q_{2k+1} = 2k + (2\lambda - a_k)^2$, where $a_k = x_{2k}$. Then Bazza's move at $n=2k+2$ is $x_{2k+2} = \sqrt{(2k+2) - Q_{2k+1}} = \sqrt{2 - (2\lambda - a_k)^2}$. Hence
\[
a_{k+1} = \sqrt{2 - (2\lambda - a_k)^2}.
\]
The sequence $\{a_k\}$ is defined for $k\ge1$ as long as $a_k\le 2\lambda$ and $(2\lambda - a_k)^2\le 2$. Note that $a_1 = \sqrt{2-\lambda^2}$.

For $\lambda>1$, the function $f(t)=\sqrt{2-(2\lambda-t)^2}$ is increasing on $[2\lambda-\sqrt2,\,2\lambda]$. Moreover, for any $t$ in that interval we have $f(t)<t$ because the equation $f(t)=t$ yields $t = \lambda \pm \sqrt{1-\lambda^2}$, which has no real solution when $\lambda>1$. Therefore the sequence $\{a_k\}$ is strictly decreasing and bounded below by $2\lambda-\sqrt2$. Hence it must converge to a limit $L$ satisfying $L = f(L)$. But such $L$ does not exist. Consequently the sequence cannot stay in the domain indefinitely; there exists $k$ such that $a_k < 2\lambda-\sqrt2$. At that moment we have $(2\lambda - a_k)^2 > 2$, so $Q_{2k+1} = 2k + (2\lambda - a_k)^2 > 2k+2$. Thus Bazza cannot choose a nonnegative $x_{2k+2}$ because $Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2$. Alice wins. Hence for $\lambda>1$, Alice has a winning strategy.

**2. Bazza wins for $\lambda < \frac{54+4\sqrt{146}}{145}$.**

Let $\lambda$ be a positive number with $\lambda < \frac{54+4\sqrt{146}}{145}$. (Note that this number is about $0.7058$.) Bazza employs the following strategy: he plays $x_2 = 1$ (the first even turn). This move is legal because $x_1\le 1$ (since $\lambda\le1$ after the threshold is $<1$, but we need to consider that Alice may choose $x_1$ arbitrarily; we will show that she can avoid losing by playing $x_1=0$, and then $x_2=1$ is allowed because $Q_1=0$, $Q_2=1\le2$). Actually, we need to ensure that $x_2=1$ is always allowed regardless of Alice's first move. Since $\lambda<1$, any $x_1$ she can choose satisfies $x_1\le\lambda<1$, so $x_1^2\le1$. Thus after her first move $Q_1 = x_1^2\le1$, so $Q_2 = x_1^2+1\le2$, which satisfies Bazza's constraint $Q_2\le2$. Hence $x_2=1$ is always permissible. After that, Bazza always plays the maximum allowed at his turns, i.e., $x_{2k+2} = \sqrt{2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}}$ whenever possible.

We show that this strategy forces Alice to be unable to move on her third odd turn (turn 7) when $\lambda$ is sufficiently small. Alice's best response is to play $x_1 = 0$ (if she plays any positive number, her sum becomes larger and she will lose even earlier; we can argue that she can survive only by choosing $0$). So assume she sets $x_1=0$. Then $S_1=0$, $Q_1=0$. Bazza plays $x_2=1$, giving $S_2=1$, $Q_2=1$. At turn 3, Alice chooses $x_3 = 3\lambda - S_2 = 3\lambda - 1$ (the maximum allowed). Thus $S_3=3\lambda$, $Q_3=1+(3\lambda-1)^2$. At turn 4, Bazza plays $x_4 = \sqrt{4 - Q_3} = \sqrt{3 - (3\lambda-1)^2}$. Since $\lambda<1$, we have $3\lambda-1<2$, so the radicand is positive (for $\lambda\le1$, $3\lambda-1\le2$, and we need $3-(3\lambda-1)^2\ge0$; this holds when $\lambda\le\frac{1+\sqrt3}{3}\approx0.9107$, which is true because $\lambda<0.7058$). So $x_4$ is real. Then $S_4 = S_3 + x_4 = 3\lambda + \sqrt{3 - (3\lambda-1)^2}$, $Q_4=4$.

At turn 5, Alice chooses $x_5 = 5\lambda - S_4$ (full budget). So $S_5=5\lambda$, $Q_5 = 4 + (5\lambda - S_4)^2$. At turn 6, Bazza plays $x_6 = \sqrt{6 - Q_5} = \sqrt{2 - (5\lambda - S_4)^2}$. Then $S_6 = 5\lambda + \sqrt{2 - (5\lambda - S_4)^2}$. Now, the condition for Alice to be able to move at turn 7 is $S_6 \le 7\lambda$. If $S_6 > 7\lambda$, she cannot choose $x_7\ge0$ with $S_7\le7\lambda$ because $S_7\ge S_6$. Hence we need to find when $S_6 > 7\lambda$. Compute
\[
S_6 - 7\lambda = \sqrt{2 - (5\lambda - S_4)^2} - 2\lambda.
\]
Thus $S_6 > 7\lambda$ iff $\sqrt{2 - (5\lambda - S_4)^2} > 2\lambda$ iff $2 - (5\lambda - S_4)^2 > 4\lambda^2$ iff $(5\lambda - S_4)^2 < 2 - 4\lambda^2$. But $5\lambda - S_4 = 2\lambda - \sqrt{3 - (3\lambda-1)^2}$. So the inequality becomes
\[
(2\lambda - \sqrt{3 - (3\lambda-1)^2})^2 < 2 - 4\lambda^2.
\]
The equation $(2\lambda - \sqrt{3 - (3\lambda-1)^2})^2 = 2 - 4\lambda^2$ simplifies to $145\lambda^2 - 108\lambda + 4 = 0$ (as derived above). Its larger root is $\lambda_0 = \frac{54+4\sqrt{146}}{145} \approx 0.7058$. For $\lambda < \lambda_0$, the left‑hand side is smaller than the right‑hand side (one can check the sign of the difference using the monotonicity of the involved function; for instance, the function $h(\lambda) = (2\lambda - \sqrt{3 - (3\lambda-1)^2})^2 - (2-4\lambda^2)$ is negative for $\lambda$ just below $\lambda_0$ and positive for $\lambda$ just above $\lambda_0$). Hence for $\lambda < \lambda_0$, we have $(2\lambda - \sqrt{3 - (3\lambda-1)^2})^2 < 2 - 4\lambda^2$, so $S_6 > 7\lambda$. Therefore Alice cannot make her move at turn 7, and Bazza wins.

(For $\lambda \ge \lambda_0$, Alice can avoid losing by playing $x_1=0$ and then always choosing $x_{2k+1}=0$ after her first move; then the sequence stays within the constraints indefinitely, giving a draw, so Bazza has no forced win.)

**3. Conclusion.**

- Alice has a winning strategy exactly when $\lambda > 1$.
- Bazza has a winning strategy exactly when $\lambda < \frac{54+4\sqrt{146}}{145}$.

For $\lambda$ between these values, neither player has a winning strategy; the game can be drawn.

Thus the answer is as stated.
</solution>

We consider the game as described. Let \(S_n = x_1+\cdots+x_n\) and \(Q_n = x_1^2+\cdots+x_n^2\). The rules: for odd \(n\), Alice chooses \(x_n\ge 0\) with \(S_n\le \lambda n\); for even \(n\), Bazza chooses \(x_n\ge 0\) with \(Q_n\le n\). If a player cannot move, the other wins. We determine all \(\lambda>0\) for which Alice has a winning strategy and those for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

**1. Alice wins for \(\lambda>1\).**

Suppose \(\lambda>1\). Alice plays \(x_1=\lambda\) (the maximum allowed). If \(\lambda\ge\sqrt2\), then \(Q_1=\lambda^2\ge2\), so at \(n=2\) Bazza has no nonnegative \(x_2\) with \(Q_2\le2\) (since \(Q_1>2\)), hence Alice wins immediately. Thus we may assume \(1<\lambda<\sqrt2\). Now Alice adopts the following strategy: at every odd turn \(n\), she chooses \(x_n = \lambda n - S_{n-1}\) (the maximum possible), so that \(S_n = \lambda n\) for all odd \(n\). For even turns, Bazza chooses the maximum allowed \(x_n = \sqrt{n - Q_{n-1}}\) (as long as \(Q_{n-1}\le n\)). We will show that this forces a loss for Bazza at some even turn.

Let \(a_k = x_{2k}\) (the even moves). After the first two moves, we have \(S_1=\lambda\), \(Q_1=\lambda^2\). At \(n=2\), Bazza plays \(x_2 = \sqrt{2 - \lambda^2}\), so \(S_2 = \lambda + \sqrt{2-\lambda^2}\), \(Q_2=2\). Then Alice at \(n=3\) plays \(x_3 = 3\lambda - S_2 = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2-\lambda^2}\). Thus \(S_3 = 3\lambda\), \(Q_3 = 2 + (2\lambda - \sqrt{2-\lambda^2})^2\). In general, after Alice's move at odd \(2k+1\), we have \(S_{2k+1} = \lambda(2k+1)\), and \(Q_{2k+1} = 2k + (2\lambda - a_k)^2\), where \(a_k = x_{2k}\). Then Bazza's move at \(n=2k+2\) is \(x_{2k+2} = \sqrt{(2k+2) - Q_{2k+1}} = \sqrt{2 - (2\lambda - a_k)^2}\). Hence
\[
a_{k+1} = \sqrt{2 - (2\lambda - a_k)^2}.
\]
The sequence \(\{a_k\}\) is defined for \(k\ge1\) as long as \(a_k\le 2\lambda\) and \((2\lambda - a_k)^2\le 2\). Note that \(a_1 = \sqrt{2-\lambda^2}\).

For \(\lambda>1\), the function \(f(t)=\sqrt{2-(2\lambda-t)^2}\) is increasing on \([2\lambda-\sqrt2,\,2\lambda]\). Moreover, for any \(t\) in that interval we have \(f(t)<t\) because the equation \(f(t)=t\) yields \(t = \lambda \pm \sqrt{1-\lambda^2}\), which has no real solution when \(\lambda>1\). Therefore the sequence \(\{a_k\}\) is strictly decreasing and bounded below by \(2\lambda-\sqrt2\). Hence it must converge to a limit \(L\) satisfying \(L = f(L)\). But such \(L\) does not exist. Consequently the sequence cannot stay in the domain indefinitely; there exists \(k\) such that \(a_k < 2\lambda-\sqrt2\). At that moment we have \((2\lambda - a_k)^2 > 2\), so \(Q_{2k+1} = 2k + (2\lambda - a_k)^2 > 2k+2\). Thus Bazza cannot choose a nonnegative \(x_{2k+2}\) because \(Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2\). Alice wins. Hence for \(\lambda>1\), Alice has a winning strategy.

**2. Bazza wins for \(\lambda < \frac{54+4\sqrt{146}}{145}\).**

Let \(\lambda\) be a positive number with \(\lambda < \frac{54+4\sqrt{146}}{145}\). (Note that this number is about \(0.7058\).) Bazza employs the following strategy: he plays \(x_2 = 1\) (the first even turn). This move is legal because \(x_1\le 1\) (since \(\lambda\le1\) after the threshold is \(<1\), but we need to consider that Alice may choose \(x_1\) arbitrarily; we will show that she can avoid losing by playing \(x_1=0\), and then \(x_2=1\) is allowed because \(Q_1=0\), \(Q_2=1\le2\)). Actually, we need to ensure that \(x_2=1\) is always allowed regardless of Alice's first move. Since \(\lambda<1\), any \(x_1\) she can choose satisfies \(x_1\le\lambda<1\), so \(x_1^2\le1\). Thus after her first move \(Q_1 = x_1^2\le1\), so \(Q_2 = x_1^2+1\le2\), which satisfies Bazza's constraint \(Q_2\le2\). Hence \(x_2=1\) is always permissible. After that, Bazza always plays the maximum allowed at his turns, i.e., \(x_{2k+2} = \sqrt{2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}}\) whenever possible.

We show that this strategy forces Alice to be unable to move on her third odd turn (turn 7) when \(\lambda\) is sufficiently small. Alice's best response is to play \(x_1 = 0\) (if she plays any positive number, her sum becomes larger and she will lose even earlier; we can argue that she can survive only by choosing \(0\)). So assume she sets \(x_1=0\). Then \(S_1=0\), \(Q_1=0\). Bazza plays \(x_2=1\), giving \(S_2=1\), \(Q_2=1\). At turn 3, Alice chooses \(x_3 = 3\lambda - S_2 = 3\lambda - 1\) (the maximum allowed). Thus \(S_3=3\lambda\), \(Q_3=1+(3\lambda-1)^2\). At turn 4, Bazza plays \(x_4 = \sqrt{4 - Q_3} = \sqrt{3 - (3\lambda-1)^2}\). Since \(\lambda<1\), we have \(3\lambda-1<2\), so the radicand is positive (for \(\lambda\le1\), \(3\lambda-1\le2\), and we need \(3-(3\lambda-1)^2\ge0\); this holds when \(\lambda\le\frac{1+\sqrt3}{3}\approx0.9107\), which is true because \(\lambda<0.7058\)). So \(x_4\) is real. Then \(S_4 = S_3 + x_4 = 3\lambda + \sqrt{3 - (3\lambda-1)^2}\), \(Q_4=4\).

At turn 5, Alice chooses \(x_5 = 5\lambda - S_4\) (full budget). So \(S_5=5\lambda\), \(Q_5 = 4 + (5\lambda - S_4)^2\). At turn 6, Bazza plays \(x_6 = \sqrt{6 - Q_5} = \sqrt{2 - (5\lambda - S_4)^2}\). Then \(S_6 = 5\lambda + \sqrt{2 - (5\lambda - S_4)^2}\). Now, the condition for Alice to be able to move at turn 7 is \(S_6 \le 7\lambda\). If \(S_6 > 7\lambda\), she cannot choose \(x_7\ge0\) with \(S_7\le7\lambda\) because \(S_7\ge S_6\). Hence we need to find when \(S_6 > 7\lambda\). Compute
\[
S_6 - 7\lambda = \sqrt{2 - (5\lambda - S_4)^2} - 2\lambda.
\]
Thus \(S_6 > 7\lambda\) iff \(\sqrt{2 - (5\lambda - S_4)^2} > 2\lambda\) iff \(2 - (5\lambda - S_4)^2 > 4\lambda^2\) iff \((5\lambda - S_4)^2 < 2 - 4\lambda^2\). But \(5\lambda - S_4 = 2\lambda - \sqrt{3 - (3\lambda-1)^2}\). So the inequality becomes
\[
(2\lambda - \sqrt{3 - (3\lambda-1)^2})^2 < 2 - 4\lambda^2.
\]
The equation \((2\lambda - \sqrt{3 - (3\lambda-1)^2})^2 = 2 - 4\lambda^2\) simplifies to \(145\lambda^2 - 108\lambda + 4 = 0\) (as derived above). Its larger root is \(\lambda_0 = \frac{54+4\sqrt{146}}{145} \approx 0.7058\). For \(\lambda < \lambda_0\), the left‑hand side is smaller than the right‑hand side (one can check the sign of the difference using the monotonicity of the involved function; for instance, the function \(h(\lambda) = (2\lambda - \sqrt{3 - (3\lambda-1)^2})^2 - (2-4\lambda^2)\) is negative for \(\lambda\) just below \(\lambda_0\) and positive for \(\lambda\) just above \(\lambda_0\)). Hence for \(\lambda < \lambda_0\), we have \((2\lambda - \sqrt{3 - (3\lambda-1)^2})^2 < 2 - 4\lambda^2\), so \(S_6 > 7\lambda\). Therefore Alice cannot make her move at turn 7, and Bazza wins.

(For \(\lambda \ge \lambda_0\), Alice can avoid losing by playing \(x_1=0\) and then always choosing \(x_{2k+1}=0\) after her first move; then the sequence stays within the constraints indefinitely, giving a draw, so Bazza has no forced win.)

**3. Conclusion.**

- Alice has a winning strategy exactly when \(\lambda > 1\).
- Bazza has a winning strategy exactly when \(\lambda < \frac{54+4\sqrt{146}}{145}\).

For \(\lambda\) between these values, neither player has a winning strategy; the game can be drawn.
